                    <button class="album__button" aria-label="Open Phoenix by Pedro The Lion" data-artist-url="Pedro+The+Lion" data-album-url="Phoenix" data-artist="Pedro The Lion" data-album="Phoenix" data-thumb="https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/13ae0d7370d6ab0e0a49f6d90fa51871.jpg" data-track="Powerful Taboo" data-review="<p>Pedro’s back! It’s been 15 years since David Bazan hung up the moniker and embarked on a solo career that explored Bazan’s musical creativity and genre-fidelity. That evolution is poured back into Pedro the Lion with a new record that explores Bazan’s early childhood in Phoenix, AZ. The record is also a scavenger hunt of references to several of his previous releases.</p>

<p>At one of Bazan’s living room shows this year, I got to hear him describe a lot of what this record meant to him. The message found in the lines of <em>Powerful Taboo</em> moved him to speak at length about how many of us are taught to view themselves as untrustworthy or sinful. The record hits me in many of the same ways that visiting my old childhood home of Safety Harbor, FL can feel, and I can relate a lot to Bazan’s wishy-washy nostalgia.</p>

                    <button class="album__button" aria-label="Open 'Til the End by MiTis" data-artist-url="MiTis" data-album-url="%27Til+the+End" data-artist="MiTis" data-album="'Til the End" data-thumb="https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/585db96a024579a469b50494cd35316d.jpg" data-track="" data-review="<p>Through the thumping crecendos and and past the creative synths, this record is very pretty and very sad. I don’t listen to a lot to EDM, but <em>‘Til the End</em> caught my attention for it’s ability to constantly pull my attention back with its narrative. Woven between instrumentals, the break-up of a relationship unfolds via MitiS’s guest singers while the tracks themselves dance around the subject at hand. The desperate bargaining of <em>By My Side</em> describes that terror of impending heartbreak, while it is harshly rebutted in <em>For Miles &amp; Miles</em> telling the listener to “Don’t think just walk away and forget me.” <em>Embers</em> ends the story with romanticized denial finally giving way to acceptance, and the record leaves us with a victorious few minutes to contemplate where we go now in <em>Dreams</em>.</p>
" data-tags="electronic, edm, concept.album" data-genius="https://genius.com/albums/Mitis/Til-the-end" data-spotify="spotify:album:09dfll1eY4PRh0nSWIuFBi" tabindex="-1" data-lastfm="https://www.last.fm/music/MitiS/%27Til+the+End" style="background-image: url(&quot;https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/585db96a024579a469b50494cd35316d.jpg&quot;);">
                        <span class="byline">
                            <span class="byline__title">'Til the End</span> by <span class="byline__artist">MiTis</span>
                        </span>
                    </button>
                </div>
            </li>

                    <button class="album__button" aria-label="Open Blue by Communions" data-artist-url="Communions" data-album-url="Blue" data-artist="Communions" data-album="Blue" data-thumb="https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/7e1b4c0ec5d501b362ecb04c55cc0636.jpg" data-track="She's a Myth" data-review="<p>After a flurry of singles and EPs over two years, Danish band Communions finally released their first full-length record <em>Blue</em>. The record carries itself along the same well-trodden path of many recent post-punk bands like <strong>The Strokes</strong>, <strong>Interpol</strong>, and <strong>The Vaccines</strong>, which themselves are step-children of many forebears. While <strong>Communions</strong> aren’t necessarily breaking new ground with <em>Blue</em>, it shouldn’t incumbent upon every act to do so. Instead I find myself welcoming a new chapter to the genre. Sometimes we all want something we recognize. In that regard, <em>Blue</em> is a finely tuned homage.</p>

<p>Singer Martin Rehof’s high-pitched wail pairs with the instrumentation on this record perfectly, with the intro-free record throwing the listener immediately into the thick of it. 40 seconds in and you are already in an ear-worm chorus that can only be removed with multiple listens.</p>

<p>Knowing that they aren’t playing anything we haven’t heard before, <strong>Communions</strong> puts a lot of love into this record, where even the more boring tracks like <em>Midnight Child</em> or <em>Got to Be Free</em> are less apparent than hits like <em>She’s a Myth</em>.</p>

Lockett Pundt produced &quot;Bicentennial Man&quot; and &quot;Go Out and Get It&quot;, while Mark Ronson was responsible for producing the rest. The production by Ronson was conducted first in a Brooklyn studios, but completed in Black Lips' hometown of Atlanta due to time constraints. The music video for the first single, &quot;Go Out and Get It&quot;, was released on March 2.                     <button class="album__button" aria-label="Open Cover the Windows and the Walls by Grouper" data-artist-url="Grouper" data-album-url="Cover+the+Windows+and+the+Walls" data-artist="Grouper" data-album="Cover the Windows and the Walls" data-thumb="https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/5fb0ed8c165249b4ab6541872242abd3.png" data-track="" data-review="<p>Liz Harris’ often overlooked 2007 album <em>Cover the Windows and the Walls</em> is a fantastic work of dreamy ambient reverb. Like every wave of sound in these collected seven tracks, the records sound bubbles up from a restful ocean that keeps you afloat for about 40 minutes before drifting away once again.</p>

<p>Grouper’s first couple of albums came to my attention on through a small ambient music file-sharing blog that I stumbled on some sleepy evening in my college dorm room. The year before, I moved from a suburban sprawl in central Florida to Jersey City, NJ, every sight was unlike anything I’d grown up around. My daily life had become a montage of newness that craved a soundtrack. Looking over the records that I listened to the most that year, including the ones listed next to this record, I listened to a lot of ambient.</p>

<p>Harris’ vocals are present but lost in the fog of sound that envelops this entire album. The only percussion is the ringing echo of the heavily blurred guitar strings being plucked. What you’re left with is a beautiful and mysterious soundscape that doesn’t lend itself to singles, but rather must be taken as a whole work.</p>

<p><em>Hello Control</em> is a hook-heavy and danceable album that is a lot of fun and delivers on every track. Each song has something to add to the album and I still keep returning to it years later.</p>

                    <button class="album__button" aria-label="Open Achilles' Heel by Pedro The Lion" data-artist-url="Pedro+The+Lion" data-album-url="Achilles%27+Heel" data-artist="Pedro The Lion" data-album="Achilles' Heel" data-thumb="https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/2cc10fd2079896f1fb61254e6d485ef6.jpg" data-track="Foregone Conclusions" data-review="<p>This album was Bazan’s farewell to the <strong>Pedro The Lion</strong> project. After years of rotating bandmates and a falling out with longtime collaborator <strong>T.W. Walsh</strong>, Bazan decided it was time to hangup the Lion. I remember feverishly reading through <em>Pitchfork Media</em> articles on a community college classroom computer in Florida at the time. Bazan’s music had become some of the most inspirational and heartfelt content I had ever heard up to that point and it was sad to think that he was ending something that had become so important to me.</p>

<p>The record itself has continued to gain favor with me even as the years pass. The tone of the album is nowhere close to the gnashing distorted guitars of <em>Control</em>; but neither is it a return to the soft, muted tones of his first two albums <em>Winners</em>, or <em>Friends</em>. The jangling guitars remind me more of the garage rock revival that was going on at the time. Here too, we can hear Bazan’s vocal prowess evolve on the album’s opening track <em>Bands With Managers</em>, where Bazan springs into a falsetto over the repeated chorus.</p>

<p>My favorite track, <em>Foregone Conclusions</em>, is a sarcastic response to his proselytizing peers that shows a Bazan–whose nuanced Christianity was already nearing its breaking point–flirting with heresy.</p>

<p>The album is a collection of dark stories that list a number of weaknesses that Bazan was struggling with; whether they be his bandmates, his faith, drinking, or love.</p>
" data-tags="indie.rock" data-genius="" data-spotify="spotify:album:6cRhboSBQIIQwuexf2OOOV" tabindex="-1" data-lastfm="https://www.last.fm/music/Pedro+the+Lion/Achilles%27+Heel" style="background-image: url(&quot;https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/2cc10fd2079896f1fb61254e6d485ef6.jpg&quot;);">

In the days that I was writing music for various bands, I longed to be able to convey an honest feeling such as James does here.</p>

<p>Throwing one’s life away for a momentary pleasure as described in <em>Traded for Fire</em> carries so much of the Christian guilt I had been raised with that I feel my hands shake when those first warbling piano keys strike, like an alarm bringing me out of a deep sleep.</p>

<p>So jealous of James’ writing was I that I once lied to an old girlfriend that I had written <em>Hannibal, MO</em> when she heard me sing it for her on a dark summer night during a power outage in Manhattan. His persistent belief that he is not worthy of redemption is so powerful here and his soft resignation to it strikes me right in my heart. A man sits in prison after the unfortunate death of his lover announces that “Her father says he’ll shoot me down, if I don’t do it myself.”</p>

Listening to them again transports you right back to that moment, and you feel everything all over again as if for the first time. This record came out right as I was experiencing my first break-up in high school. Listening to <em>Your Majesty</em> puts me right back in that desperate first heart-break and I can see myself wandering the small town in Florida I grew up in searching for an identity that hurt less. Being reminded that “I always will be haunted by the ones I’ve loved” was exactly the record I needed at that time which, along with <strong>Hot Rod Circuit’s</strong> <em>Sorry About Tomorrow</em>, allowed me to feel and preserve that experience in a way that I can nostalgically revisit with these songs.</p>

                    <button class="album__button" aria-label="Open Control by Pedro The Lion" data-artist-url="Pedro+The+Lion" data-album-url="Control" data-artist="Pedro The Lion" data-album="Control" data-thumb="https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/e1a4d20c0dd223df5eb2dfa0533da4d1.jpg" data-track="" data-review="<p>Bazan’s <em>Control</em> is one of the most influential and powerful records I’ve ever listened to. The record’s depiction of relationships and their entropic conclusions was a relatable subject during my teens as the child of bitterly divorced parents. Listening to an (at the time) Christian artist that was willing to talk about these kinds of subjects was therapeutic. I could relate to a lot of his frustrations of being a believer disillusioned with modern American Christianity and wondering what that meant (A subject further explored in his subsequent albums and career). The crunching mid-tempo droning rock is a departure for <strong>Pedro the Lion</strong> that allowed the instrumentation of his songs reflect a lot of the violence told in these songs. The brutal honesty of tragic vanity is hammered home in each track as the loose narrative of a failed businessman who destroys his marriage and family with his infidelity, a path that ultimately leads him to finally be killed by his wife during an argument. I was lucky enough to watch a reunited <strong>Pedro</strong> play the <em>Control</em> album in its entirety live in 2012 and loved watching these songs performed after spending so many evenings listening to this record.</p>

                    <button class="album__button" aria-label="Open The Rising Tide by Sunny Day Real Estate" data-artist-url="Sunny+Day+Real+Estate" data-album-url="The+Rising+Tide" data-artist="Sunny Day Real Estate" data-album="The Rising Tide" data-thumb="https://lastfm.freetls.fastly.net/i/u/300x300/8f8dcceeb6634154b80e708c77fa88a0.png" data-track="Disappear" data-review="<p>I’m of two minds when I listen to this record. It’s such a pretty album. It’s such a well produced and curated selection of late-nineties indie rock. But it’s hardly a <strong>Sunny Day Real Estate</strong> album.</p>

<p>When I listen to this album, I’m transported back in time to those awkward teenage years where everything seemed so damned important. The clothes you wore, the people you were seen with, the music you listened to–these were the currency we traded in and I was no different from my peers. Emo music was huge and a second generation of bands were taking up where bands from the 80s and the early 90s had left off. I was all over it. Anything that could have that title applied to it became fodder for my ears.</p>

<p>I believe I owe Napster a lot of credit for the music nerd I grew into, and it was there that I learned about <strong>Sunny Day Real Estate</strong>. Discovering a band that helped define the genre and shape its sound made me feel like I had some sort of secret knowledge that others didn’t. Hearing they were reunited once again and coming out with another album was amazing news.</p>

<p>My dad bought me this record. I remember sitting in my brother’s bedroom in Florida and eagerly listening to the opening crescendo of <em>Killed By An Angel</em>. But again, this is hardly an <strong>SDRE</strong> album. Enigk’s lyrics are actually discernible for once. One can immediately hear a lot of prog rock influences on this record, and I personally hear a lot of <strong>Kansas</strong>.</p>

<p>Years later, it’s not my favorite <strong>SDRE</strong> album, but it still has songs to look forward to–like <em>Disappear</em> with it’s driving beat and whining guitars–that remind me of the band I know they once were.</p>

                    </button>
                </div>
            </li>
